        <title>The miniature genome of a carnivorous plant Genlisea aurea contains a low number of genes and short non-coding sequences | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Genlisea aurea (Lentibulariaceae) is a carnivorous plant with unusually small genome size - 63.6&#160;Mb &#8211; one of the smallest known among higher plants. Data on the genome sizes and the phylogeny of Genlisea suggest that this is a derived state within the genus. Thus, G. aurea is an excellent model organism for studying evolutionary mechanisms of genome contraction. Here we report sequencing and de novo draft assembly of G. aurea genome. The assembly consists of 10,687 contigs of the total length of 43.4&#160;Mb and includes 17,755 complete and partial protein-coding genes. Its comparison with the genome of Mimulus guttatus, another representative of higher core Lamiales clade, reveals striking differences in gene content and length of non-coding regions. Genome contraction was a complex process, which involved gene loss and reduction of lengths of introns and intergenic regions, but not intron loss. The gene loss is more frequent for the genes that belong to multigenic families indicating that genetic redundancy is an important prerequisite for genome size reduction."/>

        <meta property="og:title" content="The miniature genome of a carnivorous plant Genlisea aurea contains a low number of genes and short non-coding sequences"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Genlisea aurea (Lentibulariaceae) is a carnivorous plant with unusually small genome size - 63.6&amp;nbsp;Mb – one of the smallest known among higher plants. Data on the genome sizes and the phylogeny of Genlisea suggest that this is a derived state within the genus. Thus, G. aurea is an excellent model organism for studying evolutionary mechanisms of genome contraction. Here we report sequencing and de novo draft assembly of G. aurea genome. The assembly consists of 10,687 contigs of the total length of 43.4&amp;nbsp;Mb and includes 17,755 complete and partial protein-coding genes. Its comparison with the genome of Mimulus guttatus, another representative of higher core Lamiales clade, reveals striking differences in gene content and length of non-coding regions. Genome contraction was a complex process, which involved gene loss and reduction of lengths of introns and intergenic regions, but not intron loss. The gene loss is more frequent for the genes that belong to multigenic families indicating that genetic redundancy is an important prerequisite for genome size reduction."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                            <li class="c-article-identifiers__item"><a href="#article-info" data-track="click" data-track-action="publication date" data-track-label="link">Published: <time datetime="2013-07-15" itemprop="datePublished">15 July 2013</time></a></li>
                        </ul>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">The miniature genome of a carnivorous plant <i>Genlisea aurea</i> contains a low number of genes and short non-coding sequences</h1>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Genlisea aurea</i> (Lentibulariaceae) is a carnivorous plant with unusually small genome size - 63.6 Mb – one of the smallest known among higher plants. Data on the genome sizes and the phylogeny of <i>Genlisea</i> suggest that this is a derived state within the genus. Thus, <i>G. aurea</i> is an excellent model organism for studying evolutionary mechanisms of genome contraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here we report sequencing and <i>de novo</i> draft assembly of <i>G. aurea</i> genome. The assembly consists of 10,687 contigs of the total length of 43.4 Mb and includes 17,755 complete and partial protein-coding genes. Its comparison with the genome of <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>, another representative of higher core Lamiales clade, reveals striking differences in gene content and length of non-coding regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Genome contraction was a complex process, which involved gene loss and reduction of lengths of introns and intergenic regions, but not intron loss. The gene loss is more frequent for the genes that belong to multigenic families indicating that genetic redundancy is an important prerequisite for genome size reduction.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>In spite of the similarity of basic cellular processes in eukaryotes, their genome sizes are extraordinarily variable. The question “Why are some genomes really big and others quite compact?” was listed by Science as one of 125 big questions that face scientific inquiry over the next quarter-century. Flowering plants provide an excellent opportunity to address this question [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bennett MD, Leitch IJ: Nuclear DNA amounts in angiosperms: targets, trends and tomorrow. Ann Bot. 2011, 107: 467-590. 10.1093/aob/mcq258." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2440e535">1</a>]. A monocot <i>Paris japonica</i> possesses a 150,000 Mb genome, the largest genome known [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Pellicer J, Fay MF, Leitch IJ: The largest eukaryotic genome of them all?. Bot J Linn Soc. 2010, 164: 10-15. 10.1111/j.1095-8339.2010.01072.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2440e541">2</a>]. By contrast, two carnivorous plants from the family Lentibulariaceae, <i>Genlisea margaretae</i> and <i>G. aurea</i> have genomes of only 63.4 Mb and 63.6 Mb, respectively, although genomes of some other species of these genus exceed 1,000 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Greilhuber J, Borsch T, Müller K, Worberg A, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Smallest angiosperm genomes found in Lentibulariaceae, with chromosomes of bacterial size. Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 770-777. 10.1055/s-2006-924101." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2440e551">3</a>]. Such flexibility of the genome size is of interest from both the evolutionary and functional points of view. In a model plant species, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, number of protein-coding genes is 27416 (TAIR 10) and average gene length is about 2,300 bp [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Derelle E, Ferraz C, Rombauts S, Rouzé P, Worden AZ, Robbens S, Partensky F, Degroeve S, Echeynié S, Cooke R, Saeys Y, Wuyts J, Jabbari K, Bowler C, Panaud O, Piégu B, Ball SG, Ral J-P, Bouget F-Y, Piganeau G, De Baets B, Picard A, Delseny M, Demaille J, Van de Peer Y, Moreau H: Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA. 2006, 103: 11647-11652. 10.1073/pnas.0604795103." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2440e557">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Town CD, Cheung F, Maiti R, Crabtree J, Haas BJ, Wortman JR, Hine EE, Althoff R, Arbogast TS, Tallon LJ, Vigouroux M, Trick M, Bancroft I: Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell. 2006, 18: 1348-1359. 10.1105/tpc.106.041665." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2440e560">5</a>]. This gives an estimate of genic (coding + introns + untranslated regions) fraction length close to 60 Mb. If Arabidopsis-based estimates were applicable to small-genome <i>Genlisea</i> species, this would imply that they either lost a large proportion of their genes or possess only very short intergenic regions.</p><p>Recent advances of sequencing technologies made it possible to characterize genomes of a number of angiosperm species. Most of them are from economically important species (such as rice, potato, soybean and apple). Also, the genome projects for plants of outstanding evolutionary significance such as basal angiosperm <i>Amborella</i> and basal eudicot <i>Aquilegia</i> are in progress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fang G-C, Blackmon BP, Henry DC, Staton ME, Saski CA, Hodges SA, Tomkins JP, Luo H: Genomic tools development for Aquilegia: construction of a BAC-based physical map. BMC Genomics. 2010, 11: 621-10.1186/1471-2164-11-621." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2440e575">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zuccolo A, Bowers JE, Estill JC, Xiong Z, Luo M, Sebastian A, Goicoechea JL, Collura K, Yu Y, Jiao Y, Duarte J, Tang H, Ayyampalayam S, Rounsley S, Kudrna D, Paterson AH, Pires JC, Chanderbali A, Soltis DE, Chamala S, Barbazuk B, Soltis PS, Albert VA, Ma H, Mandoli D, Banks J, Carlson JE, Tomkins J, dePamphilis CW, Wing RA, et al: A physical map for the Amborella trichopoda genome sheds light on the evolution of angiosperm genome structure. Genome Biol. 2011, 12: R48-10.1186/gb-2011-12-5-r48." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2440e578">8</a>]. Together with availability of efficient tools and databases for plant genome annotation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Conesa A, Götz S: Blast2GO: A Comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Int J Plant Genomics. 2008, 2008: 1-12." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2440e581">9</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Goodstein DM, Shu S, Howson R, Neupane R, Hayes RD, Fazo J, Mitros T, Dirks W, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Rokhsar DS: Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40: D1178-D1186. 10.1093/nar/gkr944." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2440e585">11</a>], this enables studies of the genome size evolution in angiosperms. Phylogeny of genus <i>Genlisea</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Fleischmann A, Schäferhoff B, Heubl G, Rivadavia F, Barthlott W, Müller KF: Phylogenetics and character evolution in the carnivorous plant genus Genlisea A. St.-Hil. (Lentibulariaceae). Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2010, 56: 768-783. 10.1016/j.ympev.2010.03.009." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2440e591">12</a>] implies that the small genomes of closely related <i>G. margaretae</i> and <i>G. aurea</i> is a derived condition, because the genomes of both their sister species <i>G. hispidula</i> (1,510 Mb), and outgroup species <i>G. violacea</i> (1,005 Mb), <i>G. lobata</i> (1,227 Mb), and <i>G. uncinata</i> (995 Mb) are much larger [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Greilhuber J, Borsch T, Müller K, Worberg A, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Smallest angiosperm genomes found in Lentibulariaceae, with chromosomes of bacterial size. Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 770-777. 10.1055/s-2006-924101." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2440e613">3</a>]. This indicates that after its divergence from the <i>G. hispidula,</i> genomes in small-genome <i>Genlisea</i> lineage underwent contraction by the factor of more than 10.</p><p>A variety of mechanisms can be responsible for genome contraction in the evolutionary history of <i>Genlisea</i>. First, large genome segments, perhaps even full chromosomes, could be lost. However, this is unlikely to be the case: genomes of <i>G. margaretae</i> and <i>G. aurea</i> consist of more chromosomes than <i>Genlisea</i> species with bigger genome size (40–52 vs 22–32) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Greilhuber J, Borsch T, Müller K, Worberg A, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Smallest angiosperm genomes found in Lentibulariaceae, with chromosomes of bacterial size. Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 770-777. 10.1055/s-2006-924101." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2440e637">3</a>]. Second, loss of genes could be involved. Whole-genome duplications (WGD) occurred several times during the diversification of angiosperms, leading to massive emergence of paralogous genes. Though functional divergence of duplicated genes is an important mechanism in plant evolution, in some cases the paralogs are completely or partially redundant, thus being plausible candidates for gene loss [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Town CD, Cheung F, Maiti R, Crabtree J, Haas BJ, Wortman JR, Hine EE, Althoff R, Arbogast TS, Tallon LJ, Vigouroux M, Trick M, Bancroft I: Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell. 2006, 18: 1348-1359. 10.1105/tpc.106.041665." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2440e641">5</a>]. Third, a significant part of plant genome is represented by repetitive DNA (up to 80% in grasses and conifers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hellsten U, Mitros T, Poliakov A, Schmutz J, Spannagl M, Tang H, Wang X, Wicker T, Bharti AK, Chapman J, Feltus FA, Gowik U, Grigoriev IV, Lyons E, Maher CA, Martis M, Narechania A, Otillar RP, Penning BW, Salamov AA, Wang Y, Zhang L, Carpita NC, et al: The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 2009, 457: 551-556. 10.1038/nature07723." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2440e644">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kovach A, Wegrzyn JL, Parra G, Holt C, Bruening GE, Loopstra CA, Hartigan J, Yandell M, Langley CH, Korf I, Neale DB: The Pinus taeda genome is characterized by diverse and highly diverged repetitive sequences. BMC Genomics. 2010, 11: 420-10.1186/1471-2164-11-420." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2440e647">14</a>]) and apparently could be reduced. Fourth, shrinkage of non-coding DNA is also possible: though parts of intergenic regions or introns are functionally important and have to be retained in evolution [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hong RL, Hamaguchi L, Busch MA, Weigel D: Regulatory elements of the floral homeotic gene AGAMOUS identified by phylogenetic footprinting and shadowing. Plant Cell. 2003, 15: 1296-1309. 10.1105/tpc.009548." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2440e650">15</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shabalina SA, Ogurtsov AY, Kondrashov VA, Kondrashov AS: Selective constraint in intergenic regions of human and mouse genomes. Trends Genet. 2001, 17: 373-376. 10.1016/S0168-9525(01)02344-7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2440e653">17</a>], a large proportion of non-coding sequences appear to be dispensable.</p><p>Recently, the genome of <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, a close relative of model plant species <i>A. thaliana</i>, has been sequenced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hu TT, Pattyn P, Bakker EG, Cao J, Cheng J-F, Clark RM, Fahlgren N, Fawcett JA, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hollister JD, Ossowski S, Ottilar RP, Salamov AA, Schneeberger K, Spannagl M, Wang X, Yang L, Nasrallah ME, Bergelson J, Carrington JC, Gaut BS, Schmutz J, Mayer KFX, Van de Peer Y, Grigoriev IV, Nordborg M, Weigel D, Guo Y-L: The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat Genet. 2011, 43: 476-481. 10.1038/ng.807." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2440e665">18</a>]. <i>A. lyrata</i> genome is about 1.7 times larger than <i>A. thaliana</i> genome. Comparison of these genomes revealed that the difference in their sizes was mostly due to small deletions in non-coding DNA. The gene number in <i>A. lyrata</i> is also a little higher than in <i>A. thaliana</i>. This suggests that gene losses occurred during the recent evolution of <i>A. thaliana</i>, assuming that its small genome is a derived state.</p><p>Besides minimization, the genomes of <i>Genlisea</i> were reported to have another peculiar feature, an increased rate of nucleotide substitution [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jobson RW, Albert VA: Molecular rates parallel diversification contrasts between carnivorous plant sister lineages. Cladistics. 2002, 18: 453-453. 10.1016/S0748-3007(02)00009-9." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2440e691">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Müller KF, Borsch T, Legendre L, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Recent progress in understanding the evolution of carnivorous Lentibulariaceae (Lamiales). Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 748-757. 10.1055/s-2006-924706." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2440e694">20</a>]. However, this report is based mostly on the investigation of plastid DNA sequences and only one nuclear gene – that of 5.8S rRNA – was sampled. Recently the increased nucleotide substitution rate was reported for a related species from Lentibulariaceae, <i>Utricularia gibba</i>, which also has a small genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ibarra-Laclette E, Albert VA, Pérez-Torres CA, Zamudio-Hernández F, de J Ortega-Estrada M, Herrera-Estrella A, Herrera-Estrella L: Transcriptomics and molecular evolutionary rate analysis of the bladderwort (Utricularia), a carnivorous plant with a minimal genome. BMC Plant Biol. 2011, 11: 101-10.1186/1471-2229-11-101." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2440e700">21</a>]. A hypothesis based on the mutagenic action of reactive oxygen species was proposed to explain both high substitution rate and changes in the genome size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Albert VA, Jobson RW, Michael TP, Taylor DJ: The carnivorous bladderwort (Utricularia, Lentibulariaceae): a system inflates. J Exp Bot. 2010, 61: 5-9. 10.1093/jxb/erp349." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2440e704">22</a>]. Characterization of <i>G. aurea</i> genome makes it possible to reassess this hypothesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>G. aurea</i>genome: sequencing, assembly and validation</h3><p>Sequencing of the <i>G. aurea</i> genome was performed on the Illumina HiSeq2000 machine, using two paired-end libraries with average insert sizes 213 and 423. About 347 millions of paired quality-filtered reads were obtained. The reads were assembled using the CLC Genomics Workbench 5.0.1. The distribution of coverage of the assembly has two modes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM1">1</a>). The lower mode is primarily due to contaminating DNA. <i>Genlisea,</i> like members of its sister genus <i>Utricularia</i>, live in close association with microbial community [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Albert VA, Jobson RW, Michael TP, Taylor DJ: The carnivorous bladderwort (Utricularia, Lentibulariaceae): a system inflates. J Exp Bot. 2010, 61: 5-9. 10.1093/jxb/erp349." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2440e737">22</a>], and, because whole plants were used for DNA extraction, a small amount of DNA from periphyton was also present. To exclude the contigs derived from contaminating organisms we performed a two-step selection based on a read depth and similarity to known flowering-plant sequences. First, following an approach developed in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Nederbragt AJ, Rounge TB, Kausrud KL, Jakobsen KS: Identification and quantification of genomic repeats and sample contamination in assemblies of 454 pyrosequencing reads. Sequencing. 2010, 2010: 1-12." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2440e741">23</a>] for nonaxenic cultures and the observation that contamination was an important issue mostly for the lower-coverage contigs (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig1">1</a>), we removed contigs which have very low coverage (&lt;75). This resulted in a set of 11,261 contigs covering 46 Mb. Thus, we further filtered this set according to either similarity to the known DNA sequences from <i>Magnoliophyta</i> or to the absence of similarity to any known sequences (see Methods). As a result, we obtained the final high-quality set of 10,687 contigs (lengths above 1000 nt) covering 43.4 Mb or 68% of the genome (N50 = 5,786). This proportion is similar to that reported for other plant genome sequencing projects where only (or predominantly) Illumina technology was used [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Al-Dous EK, George B, Al-Mahmoud ME, Al-Jaber MY, Wang H, Salameh YM, Al-Azwani EK, Chaluvadi S, Pontaroli AC, DeBarry J, Arondel V, Ohlrogge J, Saie IJ, Suliman-Elmeer KM, Bennetzen JL, Kruegger RR, Malek JA: De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 29: 521-527. 10.1038/nbt.1860." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2440e750">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Van Bakel H, Stout JM, Cote AG, Tallon CM, Sharpe AG, Hughes TR, Page JE: The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 2011, 12: R102-10.1186/gb-2011-12-10-r102." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2440e753">25</a>]. Application of the CEGMA pipeline for assessing the resulting gene space [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Ning Z, Keane T, Korf I: Assessing the gene space in draft genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009, 37: 289-297. 10.1093/nar/gkn916." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2440e756">26</a>] showed that 187 or 75.4% of the 248 low-copy Core Eukaryotic Genes are fully present in the assembly, and 230 or 92.7% are present fully or in part. Thus, apparently our assembly covers most of the coding region of <i>G. aurea</i> genome. To assess the effects of contamination on the assembly and on our estimates of gene number we performed the test using <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> sequence data. We assembled <i>de novo</i> two sets of reads – “clean”, that contained only <i>Arabidopsis</i> reads and “contaminated” that contained also reads from other organisms and then mapped them on reference genome. Among 120 Mbp of all reference chromosomes 22 Mbp (18%) were uncovered in clean dataset and 30 Mbp (25%) were uncovered in contaminated dataset. Among 28,775 genes of reference annotation 3,850 genes (13%) were classified as uncovered in clean dataset and 4,898 genes (17%) were classified as uncovered in contaminated dataset. This shows that, first, contamination has unfavorable effect on assembly, but this effect is not dramatic, second, that non-coding regions are more likely not to be represented in the assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>Total length of</b> <b><i>Genlisea aurea</i></b> <b>contigs, split into four bins according to the coverage.</b> For each bin, the total lengths of contigs with the best BLAST hits in different groups of species are shown by color <b>(A)</b>. Relative contributions of contigs with the best BLAST hits in different groups of species to the total lengths of contigs with different coverages <b>(B)</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transcriptomes of <i>Utricularia intermedia</i> and <i>Pinguicula vulgaris</i></h3><p>In order to gain insight into evolution of the <i>G. aurea</i> genome and to improve its annotation with transcriptomic data, we performed cDNA sequencing for two related species, <i>U. intermedia</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, representing two other genera of the family Lentibulariaceae. About 30 millions of reads of 109 nt in length were obtained for each species (23 and 24 millions after trimming). <i>De novo</i> assembly resulted in 40,041 and 42,824 contigs with N50 = 853 and N50 = 1,043 for <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>U. intermedia</i>, respectively. Out of them, 32,096 and 35,752 had significant BLAST hits; taxonomic distribution of best hits is similar to that of <i>G. aurea</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Characteristics of <i>G. aurea</i>genome</h3><p>The average GC-content of the <i>G. aurea</i> genome is 40%. Standard deviation of the GC-content of a contig, 0.064, is much higher than expected under uniform distribution of nucleotides, 0.01 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM3">3</a>). There is a negative correlation of intron length with GC-content (ρ = -0.29, Spearman’s test, p &lt; 0.01).</p><p><i>De novo</i> gene prediction for the nuclear genome assembly was performed using four different methods (see Methods) and resulted in17,755 gene models (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Tab1">1</a>). Average number of exons per gene is 4.5, average length of gene including introns is 1,433 nt, average transcript length is 965 nt. Out of 17,755 gene models, 15,361 have significant BLAST hits to UniProt with similarity &gt;30%. The species that provided most top-hits is <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, followed by <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Ricinus communis</i>. Such taxonomic distribution of top-hit species is similar to that in many other plant transcriptomes. Pfam-domains were found in 13219 proteins. Mean intron length is 134 nt, and median intron length is 89 nt (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM4">4</a>). Out of 17,755 predicted transcripts, 13,279 were GO-annotated. The distribution of GO-categories in <i>Genlisea</i> is similar to <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig2">2</a>). <i>G. aurea</i> genome is characterized by a strong codon bias, with the effective number of codons used being equal to 57. GC-content is 55% in third codon position compared to only 34% in intergenic regions.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                          <b>Number of genes predicted in each annotation (aug – AUGUSTUS, gm – GeneMark-ES, gs1 – GENESEQER with</b>
                          <b><i>Utricularia intermedia</i></b>
                          <b>gs2 - GENESEQER with</b>
                          <b><i>Pinguicula vulgaris</i></b>
                          <b>, gw1 – GENEWISE with</b>
                          <b><i>Mimulus guttatus,</i></b>
                          <b>gw2 – GENEWISE with</b>
                          <b><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></b>
                          <b>, gw3 - GENEWISE with</b>
                          <b><i>Solanum lycopersicum,</i></b>
                          <b>gw4 – GENEWISE with all Uniprot proteins) and number of genes in final dataset</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>
                          <b>Distribution of GO-categories for biological process in</b>
                          <b><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></b>
                          <b>(left) and</b>
                          <b><i>Genlisea aurea</i></b>
                          <b>(right).</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative genome analysis</h3><p>The closest to <i>G. aurea</i> species with known genome is <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (<a href="http://www.phytozome.org/mimulus.php">http://www.phytozome.org/mimulus.php</a>). <i>Mimulus</i> belongs to the family Scrophulariaceae, which is, together with Lentibulariaceae, a representative of Higher Core Lamiales clade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Schäferhoff B, Fleischmann A, Fischer E, Albach DC, Borsch T, Heubl G, Müller KF: Towards resolving Lamiales relationships: insights from rapidly evolving chloroplast sequences. BMC Evol Biol. 2010, 10: 352-10.1186/1471-2148-10-352." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1349">27</a>]. Thus, in comparative analyses, we used <i>M. guttatus</i> genome as a sister to <i>G. aurea</i>, and the next closest genome of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> as an outgroup for this sister pair. Genome assembly sizes and proportions of exonic, intronic and intergenic sequences for these three species are shown in Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig3">3</a>. <i>G. aurea</i> genome has a 2.4 times smaller total exonic sequence length, 4.0 times smaller total intronic sequence length, and 13.0 times smaller total intergenic sequence length, as compared to <i>M. guttatus</i>. As for the number of protein-coding genes, 17,755 were found in our assembly. Even taking into account that 10-20% of genes could have been missed due to incomplete assembly, an estimated total number of genes is much lower in <i>Genlisea</i> than in other known angiosperm genomes. <i>M. guttatus</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> genomes contain 28,282 and 34,257 annotated genes, respectively. Therefore, a smaller number of genes in small genome <i>Genlisea</i> lineage is likely due to gene loss, which occurred since its divergence from the <i>M. guttatus</i> lineage. To test our hypothesis of dramatic gene loss we performed analysis of the genome regions which are orthologous between <i>Genlisea</i> and <i>Mimulus</i>. We considered an exon in the <i>G. aurea</i> genome to be orthologous to an exon in <i>M. guttatus</i> if they satisfy the best bidirectional (TBLASTN-BLASTX) hit criterion. A genome region between two pairs of orthologous exons was also considered as orthologous. We looked for <i>G. aurea</i> contigs such that the first and the last gene within them had orthologs in the same <i>M. guttatus</i> contig. Then, the gene content of sequence segments between these two pair of orthologs was compared for <i>G. aurea</i> and <i>M. guttatus</i>. Such segments in <i>M. guttatus</i> totally contained 2,801 genes, but for <i>G. aurea</i> the corresponding number is only 393. Out of 2,408 <i>M. guttatus</i> genes with no collinear ortholog in <i>G. aurea</i>, 961 were found to be transposed in <i>G. aurea</i> to the other genome regions, whereas 1,477 were apparently lost because either no significant BLAST hit was found or the targeted locus had better similarity to another protein-coding gene in <i>M. guttatus</i>. This analysis implies that the observed contraction of the overall length of coding sequences was due to complete loss of a fraction of genes rather than to the shortening of exons. Indeed, there was only a minimal shrinkage of individual genes, as the overall lengths of orthologous exons are very close in the two species: 11.1 Mb for <i>G. aurea</i> and 11.3 Mb for <i>M. guttatus</i>. Comparison of orthologous intergenic regions reveals shortening similar to contraction observed at the whole-genome level: 673 kb in <i>G. aurea vs.</i> 2,744 kb in <i>M. guttatus</i> (4.1 times contraction). If we consider only orthologous introns, defined as introns flanked by orthologous exons, their overall lengths are 2.7 Mb in <i>G. aurea vs.</i> 6.3 Mb in <i>M. guttatus</i>, a 2.4-fold reduction.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>
                          <b>Genome assembly size (A) and distribution of exon, intron and intergenic sequence lengths for</b>
                          <b><i>Genlisea aurea</i></b>
                          <b>,</b>
                          <b><i>Mimulus guttatus</i></b>
                          <b>and</b>
                          <b><i>Solanum lycopersicum</i></b>
                          <b>(B).</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of gene families using OrthoMCL indicates that a gene in <i>M. guttatus</i> is more likely to be absent in <i>G. aurea</i> if it has paralogs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM5">5</a>). Overall, the <i>G. aurea</i> genome is biased to genes with lesser number of copies compared e.g. to genomes of <i>M. guttatus</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig4">4</a>). The analysis of enrichment by specific GO categories revealed that protein kinases which are a large multigenic superfamily - are preferentially lost in the <i>G. aurea</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#MOESM6">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>
                          <b>Number of genes per gene family in</b>
                          <b><i>Genlisea aurea</i></b>
                          <b>and other plant species as assessed by OrthoMCL.</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Because the reduction of the genome size in <i>G. aurea</i> lineage occurred rapidly, it is natural to assume that fixations of long deletions played a role in this process. Unfortunately, due to large evolutionary distance of <i>G</i>. <i>aurea</i> and <i>M. guttatus</i> their orthologous intergenic regions are hard to align. We were able to do it only using an interactive software tool OWEN [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ogurtsov AY, Roytberg MA, Shabalina SA, Kondrashov AS: OWEN: aligning long collinear regions of genomes. Bioinformatics. 2002, 18: 1703-1704. 10.1093/bioinformatics/18.12.1703." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1571">28</a>]. In twenty pairs of randomly chosen intergenic regions, we detected 31 localized length differences longer than 500 nucleotides between the two genomes. These differences are likely due to deletions in the <i>G. aurea</i> genome, although insertions in the <i>M. guttatus</i> genome also cannot be ruled out, because intergenic regions of the outgroup genome of <i>S. lycopersicum</i> are mostly unalignable with both sister genomes and thus do not allow polarization of this character. Six out of these putative deletions were likely associated with direct, low-complexity repeats which could mediate their origin in the ancestral genome.</p><p>According to previous observations, <i>Genlisea</i> and <i>Utricularia</i> have some of the highest rates of evolution in angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jobson RW, Albert VA: Molecular rates parallel diversification contrasts between carnivorous plant sister lineages. Cladistics. 2002, 18: 453-453. 10.1016/S0748-3007(02)00009-9." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1593">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Muller K, Borsch T, Legendre L, Porembski S, Theisen I, Barthlott W: Evolution of carnivory in Lentibulariaceae and the Lamiales. Plant Biol. 2004, 6: 477-490. 10.1055/s-2004-817909." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1596">29</a>]. Possible explanations of this fact are relaxed selection in this clade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Müller KF, Borsch T, Legendre L, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Recent progress in understanding the evolution of carnivorous Lentibulariaceae (Lamiales). Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 748-757. 10.1055/s-2006-924706." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1599">20</a>] and increased mutation rate due to reactive oxygen species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Albert VA, Jobson RW, Michael TP, Taylor DJ: The carnivorous bladderwort (Utricularia, Lentibulariaceae): a system inflates. J Exp Bot. 2010, 61: 5-9. 10.1093/jxb/erp349." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1603">22</a>]. We calculated evolutionary distances at synonymous and nonsynonmous sites between <i>G. aurea</i>, <i>U. intermedia</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and <i>M. guttatus</i> using PAML v.5.0, with <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> used as an outgroup to root the tree (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig5">5</a>). Distances in the two trees are mostly proportional, and dN/dS ratio for <i>G. aurea</i>, <i>U. intermedia</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and <i>M. guttatus</i> lineages are 0.08, 0.12, 0.13, and 0.10 respectively. Thus, we see no evidence of reduced stringency of selection in the <i>Genlisea</i> + <i>Urticularia</i> clade.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-476/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5207_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>
                          <b>Phylogenetic trees for</b>
                          <b><i>Genlisea aurea</i></b>
                          <b>and related species based on synonymous (A) and non-synonymous (B) substitutions.</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion and conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion and conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>A number of features of the smallest known angiosperm genome of <i>Genlisea aurea</i> are worth mentioning. First, the GC-content of <i>G. aurea</i> genome is highly variable along its length. This is likely due to non-uniform recombination rate, which can affect GC-content due to weak selection and/or biased gene conversion. Indeed, GC-content is higher in regions with high recombination rates in both metazoa [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Jensen-Seaman MI, Furey TS, Payseur BA, Lu Y, Roskin KM, Chen C-F, Thomas MA, Haussler D, Jacob HJ: Comparative recombination rates in the rat, mouse, and human genomes. Genome Res. 2004, 14: 528-538. 10.1101/gr.1970304." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1686">30</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Beye M, Gattermeier I, Hasselmann M, Gempe T, Schioett M, Baines JF, Schlipalius D, Mougel F, Emore C, Rueppell O, Sirviö A, Guzmán-Novoa E, Hunt G, Solignac M, Page RE: Exceptionally high levels of recombination across the honey bee genome. Genome Res. 2006, 16: 1339-1344. 10.1101/gr.5680406." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1689">32</a>] and in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Paape T, Zhou P, Branca A, Briskine R, Young N, Tiffin P: Fine-scale population recombination rates, hotspots, and correlates of recombination in the Medicago truncatula genome. Genome Biol Evol. 2012, 4: 726-737. 10.1093/gbe/evs046." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1692">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gore MA, Chia J-M, Elshire RJ, Sun Q, Ersoz ES, Hurwitz BL, Peiffer JA, McMullen MD, Grills GS, Ross-Ibarra J, Ware DH, Buckler ES: A first-generation haplotype map of maize. Science. 2009, 326: 1115-1117. 10.1126/science.1177837." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1696">34</a>]. Negative correlation of intron length with GC-content is also considered to be the signature of variable recombination rates [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Comeron JM, Kreitman M: The correlation between intron length and recombination in Drosophila. Dynamic equilibrium between mutational and selective forces. Genetics. 2000, 156: 1175-1190." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1699">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Sella G, Petrov DA, Przeworski M, Andolfatto P: Pervasive natural selection in the Drosophila genome?. PLoS Genet. 2009, 5: e1000495-10.1371/journal.pgen.1000495." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1702">36</a>]. The most striking characteristic of <i>G. aurea</i> genome is that it contains low number of genes. Although we are unable to report the absolute number of genes because our assembly does not cover the genome completely, results of the search of core eukaryotic genes and of the assembly test demonstrate that we could have missed not more than 10-20 % of genes. This gives an upper estimate of gene number ~ 21 thousand – much lower than is known for any other angiosperm. Sterck et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sterck L, Rombauts S, Vandepoele K, Rouzé P, Van de Peer Y: How many genes are there in plants (… and why are they there)?. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2007, 10: 199-203. 10.1016/j.pbi.2007.01.004." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1708">37</a>] hypothesize that ancestral angiosperms could have much less genes than the recent ones, about 12–14 thousands. However, an early-branching and morphologically primitive angiosperm <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> has a standard angiosperm gene number, about 27 thousands (amborella.org). In non-flowering plants gene number is also higher: 22–35 thousand [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Rigault P, Boyle B, Lepage P, Cooke JE, Bousquet J, MacKay JJ: A white spruce gene catalog for conifer genome analyses. Plant Physiol. 2011, 157 (1): 14-28. 10.1104/pp.111.179663." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1715">38</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Rensing SA, Lang D, Zimmer AD, Terry A, Salamov A, Shapiro H, Nishiyama T, Perroud PF, Lindquist EA, Kamisugi Y, Tanahashi T, Sakakibara K, Fujita T, Oishi K, Shin-I T, Kuroki Y, Toyoda A, Suzuki Y, Hashimoto S, Yamaguchi K, Sugano S, Kohara Y, Fujiyama A, Anterola A, Aoki S, Ashton N, Barbazuk WB, Barker E, Bennetzen JL, Blankenship R, et al: The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science. 2008, 319 (5859): 64-69. 10.1126/science.1150646." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1718">40</a>]. Thus we believe that 12–14 thousands is an underestimate. A low gene number in <i>G. aurea</i> is even more surprising because this species, as well as all Lentibulariaceae species, is carnivorous. Indeed, one may expect this adaptation to depend on a number of specialized proteins. However, if carnivory results from the modification of existing metabolic pathways, instead of the appearance of the new pathways [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Spomer G: Evidence of protocarnivorous capabilities in Geranium viscosissimum and Potentilla arguta and other sticky plants. International Journal of Plant Science. 1999, 160: 98-101. 10.1086/314109." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1724">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Darnowski DW, Carroll DM, Płachno B, Kabanoff E, Cinnamon E: Evidence of protocarnivory in triggerplants (Stylidium spp.; Stylidiaceae). Plant Biol (Stuttg). 2006, 8: 805-812. 10.1055/s-2006-924472." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1727">42</a>], this expectation is wrong and carnivory can evolve without any expansion of the gene repertoire. <i>G. aurea</i> genome is one of the first characterized genomes from carnivorous plants (during revision of this manuscript the article reporting the genome of another carnivorous plant, <i>Utricularia gibba</i>, was published [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ibarra-Laclette E, Lyons E, Hernández-Guzmán G, Pérez-Torres CA, Carretero-Paulet L, Chang TH, Lan T, Welch AJ, Juárez MJ, Simpson J, Fernández-Cortés A, Arteaga-Vázquez M, Góngora-Castillo E, Acevedo-Hernández G, Schuster SC, Himmelbauer H, Minoche AE, Xu S, Lynch M, Oropeza-Aburto A, Cervantes-Pérez SA, de Jesús O-EM, Cervantes-Luevano JI, Michael TP, Mockler T, Bryant D, Herrera-Estrella A, Albert VA, Herrera-Estrella L: Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature. 2013, 498 (7452): 94-98. 10.1038/nature12132." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1737">43</a>]), and data on more such genomes and their comparative analysis would help to reveal molecular mechanisms of carnivory.</p><p>Besides reduction of gene number, we found that both introns and intergenic regions in the <i>G. aurea</i> genome are unusually short. In contrast, the per gene number of introns is typical for an angiosperm. Thus, the reduction of genome size in the <i>G. aurea</i> lineage was due to both gene loss and non-coding sequences shrinking, but not to intron loss. In all studies performed so far, angiosperm genome reduction not preceded by recent WGD was found to be caused by the loss of non-coding genome segments, including transposable elements [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hu TT, Pattyn P, Bakker EG, Cao J, Cheng J-F, Clark RM, Fahlgren N, Fawcett JA, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hollister JD, Ossowski S, Ottilar RP, Salamov AA, Schneeberger K, Spannagl M, Wang X, Yang L, Nasrallah ME, Bergelson J, Carrington JC, Gaut BS, Schmutz J, Mayer KFX, Van de Peer Y, Grigoriev IV, Nordborg M, Weigel D, Guo Y-L: The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat Genet. 2011, 43: 476-481. 10.1038/ng.807." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1749">18</a>], and no substantial decrease in gene number has been observed. The exact mechanisms and timing of such decrease are however still unknown – the gene loss or pseudogenization could have occurred in large-genome <i>Genlisea</i> ancestor, and small-genome <i>Genlisea</i> lineage could have lost pseudogenes and other non-coding genome segments. The study of closest large-genome relatives of small-genome <i>Genlisea</i> species is necessary to test this possibility. By now the closest to <i>G. aurea</i> species with a known genome is <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>. Because the evolutionary distance between these two genomes is substantial (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#Fig5">5</a>), we do not know if gene loss in the <i>G. aurea</i> lineage involved pseudogenization followed by slow shrinking of pseudogenes similar to that observed in <i>Mycobacterium leprae</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cole ST, Eiglmeier K, Parkhill J, James KD, Thomson NR, Wheeler PR, Honoré N, Garnier T, Churcher C, Harris D, Mungall K, Basham D, Brown D, Chillingworth T, Connor R, Davies RM, Devlin K, Duthoy S, Feltwell T, Fraser A, Hamlin N, Holroyd S, Hornsby T, Jagels K, Lacroix C, Maclean J, Moule S, Murphy L, Oliver K, Quail MA, et al: Massive gene decay in the leprosy bacillus. Nature. 2001, 409: 1007-1011. 10.1038/35059006." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1778">44</a>] or occurred through long deletions. Both scenarios can occur only for genes which became functionally redundant. Because the reduction of the genome size of <i>G. aurea</i> occurred rapidly, it was likely driven by selection, instead of deletion bias in the mutation process that is thought to be one of the major determinants of genome size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Petrov DA, Sangster TA, Johnston JS, Hartl DL, Shaw KL: Evidence for DNA loss as a determinant of genome size. Science. 2000, 287: 1060-1062. 10.1126/science.287.5455.1060." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1784">45</a>]. There is an increasing evidence of that genome size is not only due to mutation bias but can also be affected by selection [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Grover CE, Wendel JF: Recent insights into mechanisms of genome size change in plants. Journal of Botany. 2010, 2010: 1-8." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1787">46</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Leushkin EV, Bazykin GA, Kondrashov AS: Strong mutational bias toward deletions in the Drosophila melanogaster genome is compensated by selection. Genome Biol Evol. 2013, 5: 514-524. 10.1093/gbe/evt021." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1790">47</a>]. Genome size is correlated with a variety of morphological traits such as seed mass [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Beaulieu JM, Moles AT, Leitch IJ, Bennett MD, Dickie JB, Knight CA: Correlated evolution of genome size and seed mass. New Phytol. 2007, 173: 422-437. 10.1111/j.1469-8137.2006.01919.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1793">48</a>], cell size and stomatal density [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Beaulieu JM, Leitch IJ, Patel S, Pendharkar A, Knight CA: Genome size is a strong predictor of cell size and stomatal density in angiosperms. New Phytol. 2008, 179: 975-986. 10.1111/j.1469-8137.2008.02528.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1797">49</a>]. Correlations between genome size and generation time and mating system are also widely discussed but are less clear. Annuals usually have smaller genomes than perennials (reviewed in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Knight CA, Molinari NA, Petrov DA: The large genome constraint hypothesis: evolution, ecology and phenotype. Ann Bot. 2005, 95 (1): 177-190. 10.1093/aob/mci011." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1800">50</a>]) though in these latter there is wider range of genome sizes that overlaps with that of annuals [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Bennett MD: Nuclear DNA, content and minimum generation time in herbaceous plants. Proc R Soc Lond B Biol Sci. 1972, 181 (63): 109-135." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1803">51</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Bennet MD: Variation in genomic form in plants and its ecological implications. New Phytol. 1987, 106: 177-200." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1806">52</a>]. Also, many outcrossing species were reported to have larger genomes than their selfing relatives [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Albach DC, Greilhuber J: Genome size variation and evolution in Veronica. Ann Bot. 2004, 94: 897-911. 10.1093/aob/mch219." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1809">53</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wright SI, Ness RW, Foxe JP, Barrett SCH: Genomic consequences of outcrossing and selfing in plants. Int J Plant Sci. 2008, 169 (1): 105-118. 10.1086/523366." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1812">54</a>] however recent broad-scale comparative analysis suggests that phylogenetic signal could substantially affect this correlation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Whitney KD, Baack EJ, Hamrick JL, Godt MJ, Barringer BC, Bennett MD, Eckert CG, Goodwillie C, Kalisz S, Leitch IJ, Ross-Ibarra J: A role for nonadaptive processes in plant genome size evolution?. Evolution. 2010, 64 (7): 2097-2109." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1816">55</a>]. Vinogradov [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Vinogradov AE: Selfish DNA is maladaptive: evidence from the plant Red List. Trends Genet. 2003, 19: 609-614. 10.1016/j.tig.2003.09.010." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1819">56</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vinogradov AE: Genome size and extinction risk in vertebrates. Proc. Biol. Sci. 2004, 271: 1701-1705. 10.1098/rspb.2004.2776." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1822">57</a>] demonstrated, on both plants and animals, that threatened species tend to have larger genomes than their secure relatives (i.e. there is a correlation between the genome size and likelihood of extinction); and vice versa, reduction of genome size correlates with the invasive ability [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Lavergne S, Muenke NJ, Molofsky J: Genome size reduction can trigger rapid phenotypic evolution in invasive plants. Ann Bot. 2010, 105: 109-116. 10.1093/aob/mcp271." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1825">58</a>]. On the other hand, the reduction of genome size could reduce phenotypic plasticity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yotoko KSC, Dornelas MC, Togni PD, Fonseca TC, Salzano FM, Bonatto SL, Freitas LB: Does variation in genome sizes reflect adaptive or neutral processes? New clues from Passiflora. PLoS One. 2011, 6: e18212-10.1371/journal.pone.0018212." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1828">59</a>].</p><p>There are two mechanisms that are thought to be major driving forces of genome reduction, unequal homologous recombination and illegitimate recombination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Bennetzen JL: Mechanisms and rates of genome expansion and contraction in flowering plants. Genetica. 2002, 115: 29-36. 10.1023/A:1016015913350." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1834">60</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bennetzen JL, Ma J, Devos KM: Mechanisms of recent genome size variation in flowering plants. Ann Bot. 2005, 95: 127-132. 10.1093/aob/mci008." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1837">61</a>]. But irrespectively of the mechanism, sequences under negative selection are unlikely to be lost. Thus, one may expect the genome contraction to proceed primarily through the removal of non-functional regions (sometimes referred to as “junk DNA”). One possible cause of such removal may be an increase of the effective population size, assuming that getting rid of “junk” DNA is advantageous [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Lynch M: The Origins of Genome Architecture. 2007, Sinauer Associates Inc, 1" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1840">62</a>]. However in plants the relationship between genome size and effective population size is yet to be clarified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Whitney KD, Baack EJ, Hamrick JL, Godt MJ, Barringer BC, Bennett MD, Eckert CG, Goodwillie C, Kalisz S, Leitch IJ, Ross-Ibarra J: A role for nonadaptive processes in plant genome size evolution?. Evolution. 2010, 64 (7): 2097-2109." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1843">55</a>]. The increased strength of selection favoring reduced genome size is another possibility. Recent study of the genome size change in the genus <i>Arabidopsis</i> demonstrates that long (&gt;5 bp) deletions are selectively favored in <i>A. thaliana</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hu TT, Pattyn P, Bakker EG, Cao J, Cheng J-F, Clark RM, Fahlgren N, Fawcett JA, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hollister JD, Ossowski S, Ottilar RP, Salamov AA, Schneeberger K, Spannagl M, Wang X, Yang L, Nasrallah ME, Bergelson J, Carrington JC, Gaut BS, Schmutz J, Mayer KFX, Van de Peer Y, Grigoriev IV, Nordborg M, Weigel D, Guo Y-L: The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat Genet. 2011, 43: 476-481. 10.1038/ng.807." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1853">18</a>]. A similar process could be driving the genome contraction in <i>G. aurea</i>. As already mentioned above, short life cycle and self-pollination are important factors in the reduction of genome size. <i>G. aurea</i> is a perennial plant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Fischer E, Porembski S, Barthlott W: Revision of the genus Genlisea (Lentibulariaceae) in Africa and Madagascar with notes on ecology and phytogeography. Nord J Bot. 2000, 20: 291-318. 10.1111/j.1756-1051.2000.tb00746.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1862">63</a>]; as for the breeding system, though insect visitation of <i>G. aurea</i> flowers is documented, there are no direct evidences of cross-pollination. The results of crossing experiments on <i>Genlisea</i> species grown in cultivation demonstrated that some members of the genus are facultative autogamous [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Fleischmann A: Dissertation, LMU München: Faculty of Biology. Phylogenetic relationships, systematics, and biology of carnivorous Lamiales, with special focus on the genus Genlisea (Lentibulariaceae). 2012, &#xA;                    http://edoc.ub.uni-muenchen.de/14964/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1872">64</a>]. If <i>G. aurea</i> is self-pollinated, in contrast to its large-genome relatives, this transition to selfing could have contributed into the reduction of genome size.</p><p>While this paper was in review, a description of the 82 Mb genome of <i>Utricularia gibba</i> has been published [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ibarra-Laclette E, Lyons E, Hernández-Guzmán G, Pérez-Torres CA, Carretero-Paulet L, Chang TH, Lan T, Welch AJ, Juárez MJ, Simpson J, Fernández-Cortés A, Arteaga-Vázquez M, Góngora-Castillo E, Acevedo-Hernández G, Schuster SC, Himmelbauer H, Minoche AE, Xu S, Lynch M, Oropeza-Aburto A, Cervantes-Pérez SA, de Jesús O-EM, Cervantes-Luevano JI, Michael TP, Mockler T, Bryant D, Herrera-Estrella A, Albert VA, Herrera-Estrella L: Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature. 2013, 498 (7452): 94-98. 10.1038/nature12132." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1884">43</a>]. There are several common features between this genome and that of <i>G. aurea</i>, although they underwent miniaturization independently. In particular, both genomes have a reduced number of protein-coding genes, and the gene densities (28 per 82 in <i>U. gibba</i> vs. 21 per 64 in <i>G. aurea</i>) are rather similar, suggesting that there may be a minimal complement of non-coding sequences (1,500 nucleotides) per gene in angiosperms.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Origin, cultivation, sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The plants were cultivated in the private collection of carnivorous plants (A. Seredin, Moscow, Russia). Before DNA extraction, plants were put into distilled water and starved for two days. Total genomic DNA was extracted using modified CTAB-method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Doyle J, Doyle J: A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochemical Bulletin. 1987, 19: 11-15." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1908">65</a>]. To construct the libraries for whole genome sequencing DNA was processed as described in the TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Guide (Illumina). Two libraries with average length of 413 and 623 bp were selected for sequencing. Libraries were quantified using fluorimetry with Qubit (Invitrogen, USA) and real-time PCR and diluted up to final concentration of 8 pM. Diluted libraries were clustered on two lanes (one library per lane) of a paired-end flowcell using cBot instrument and sequenced using HiSeq2000 sequencer with TruSeq SBS Kit v3-HS (Illumina, USA). Raw reads in fastq format (about 347 millions in total) were imported into CLC Genomic Workbench program; after trimming of adapter-derived and low (Q-score below 30) quality sequences they were assembled using built-in de novo assembly application (k-mer size 64, bubble size 1,000). To minimize the presence of contigs derived from contamination (symbiotic bacteria, prey organisms) we have taken for subsequent analysis only the contigs with average coverage more than 75×. Due to several limitations RNA extraction from <i>G. aurea</i> itself was not feasible with the material that we had. Thus to improve the annotation of its genome we sequenced transcriptomes of two related species, <i>Pinguicula vulgaris</i> and <i>Utricularia intermedia</i>. The samples were taken from Moscow State University botanical garden. Total RNA was extracted from leaves using Qiagen RNEasy Plant Mini kit. About 1 microgram of total RNA was processed using TruSeq RNA Sample Preparation Guide (Illumina). Libraries were sequenced on a single-read flowcell with the read length 100 bp using HiSeq2000 instrument and TruSeq SBS Kit v3-HS (Illumina). Reads were trimmed and assembled using CLC Genomics Workbench 5.0.1 with word size = 36 and bubble size = 2,500.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Taxonomic filtering for contamination</h3><p>Contigs with read coverage greater than 75 were compared against the nt database (downloaded on December 29, 2011) using translated-query translated-databased BLAST (tblastx) with e-value cutoff 10<sup>-6</sup> and default options otherwise. Contigs were selected as passing the taxonomy filter if their top TBLASTX hit (according to e-value) belonged to <i>Magnoliophyta</i>, or they had no TBLASTX hits with this cutoff.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Nuclear genome annotation</h3><p>Assembled contigs were subject to repeat identification using program RepeatMasker (v. open-3.3.0, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R, Green P: RepeatMasker Open-3.0. 1996, &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  , –2010," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-476#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d2440e1941">66</a>]) using Embryophyta repeats (6.0% of genome were masked) and de novo repeat search tool RepeatScout [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA: De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Proceedings of the 13 annual international conference on intelligent systems for molecular biology (ISMB-05) 2005, Detroit, Michigan.
